/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>British Airways</b> (<b>BA</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/Flag_carrier" title="Flag carrier">flag carrier</a> airline of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. It is headquartered in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, England, near its main <a href="/wiki/Airline_hub" title="Airline hub">hub</a> at <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">Heathrow Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The airline is the second largest UK based carrier, based on fleet size and passengers carried, behind <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">easyJet</a>. In January 2011 BA merged with <a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a>, creating the <a href="/wiki/International_Airlines_Group" title="International Airlines Group">International Airlines Group</a> (IAG), a <a href="/wiki/Holding_company" title="Holding company">holding company</a> registered in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, Spain. IAG is the world\'s third-largest airline group in terms of annual revenue and the second-largest in Europe. It is listed on the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100_Index" title="FTSE 100 Index">FTSE 100 Index</a>. British Airways is the first passenger airline to have generated more than <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">$</a>1 billion on a single air route in a year (from 1 April 2017, to 31 March 2018, on the <a href="/wiki/London_to_New_York_Air_Route" title="London to New York Air Route">New York-JFK - London-Heathrow</a> route).<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>BA was created in 1974 after a British Airways Board was established by the British government to manage the two nationalised airline corporations, <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">British Overseas Airways Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">British European Airways</a>, and two regional airlines, <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Airways" title="Cambrian Airways">Cambrian Airways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Airlines_(UK)" title="Northeast Airlines (UK)">Northeast Airlines</a>. On 31 March 1974, all four companies were merged to form British Airways. However, it marked 2019 as its centenary based on predecessor companies.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> After almost 13 years as a state company, BA was privatised in February 1987 as part of a wider privatisation plan by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Conservative government</a>. The carrier expanded with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/British_Caledonian" title="British Caledonian">British Caledonian</a> in 1987, <a href="/wiki/Dan-Air" title="Dan-Air">Dan-Air</a> in 1992, and <a href="/wiki/British_Midland_International" title="British Midland International">British Midland International</a> in 2012. Its pre-eminence highlights the reach of the country\'s influence as many of its destinations in several regions were historically part of the British Empire.\n</p><p>It is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Oneworld" title="Oneworld">Oneworld</a> airline alliance, along with <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a>, and the now-defunct <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Airlines" title="Canadian Airlines">Canadian Airlines</a>. The alliance has since grown to become the third-largest, after <a href="/wiki/SkyTeam" title="SkyTeam">SkyTeam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Star_Alliance" title="Star Alliance">Star Alliance</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" services</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Short_haul"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Short haul</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#Economy_class"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Economy class</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-24"><a href="#Business_class"><span class="tocnumber">6.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Business class</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Mid-haul_and_long_haul"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Mid-haul and long haul</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-26"><a href="#First_class"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">First class</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27"><a href="#Club_World"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Club World</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-28"><a href="#World_Traveller_Plus"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">World Traveller Plus</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-29"><a href="#World_Traveller"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.4</span> <span class="toctext">World Traveller</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Incidents_and_accidents"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Incidents and accidents</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_British_Airways" title="History of British Airways">History of British Airways</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_747-100_in_BOAC_basic_livery_Marmet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-100" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 747-100">Boeing 747-100</a> in BOAC-British Airways transition livery (1976)</div></div></div>\n<p>Proposals to establish a joint British airline, combining the assets of the <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">British Overseas Airways Corporation</a> (BOAC) and <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">British European Airways</a> (BEA) were first raised in 1953 as a result of difficulties in attempts by BOAC and BEA to negotiate air rights through the British colony of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Increasingly BOAC was protesting that BEA was using its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_Airways" title="Cyprus Airways">Cyprus Airways</a> to circumvent an agreement that BEA would not fly routes further east than Cyprus, particularly to the increasingly important oil regions in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. The chairman of BOAC, Miles Thomas, was in favour of a merger as a potential solution to this disagreement and had backing for the idea from the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> at the time, <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a>. However, opposition from the Treasury blocked the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Consequently, it was only following the recommendations of the 1969 Edwards Report that a new British Airways Board, managing both BEA and BOAC, and the two regional British airlines <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Airways" title="Cambrian Airways">Cambrian Airways</a> based at Cardiff, and <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Airlines_(UK)" title="Northeast Airlines (UK)">Northeast Airlines</a> based at Newcastle upon Tyne, was constituted on 1 April 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Although each airline\'s branding was maintained initially, two years later the British Airways Board unified its branding, effectively establishing British Airways as an airline on 31 March 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP7_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP7-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following two years of fierce competition with <a href="/wiki/British_Caledonian" title="British Caledonian">British Caledonian</a>, the second-largest airline in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> at the time, the Government changed its aviation policy in 1976 so that the two carriers would no longer compete on long-haul routes.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways and <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">Air France</a> operated the <a href="/wiki/Supersonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> airliner <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Aerospatiale-BAC Concorde</a>, and the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Supersonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> passenger service flew in January 1976 from <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">Heathrow Airport</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_International_Airport" title="Bahrain International Airport">Bahrain International Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Concordeintro3_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Concordeintro3-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Services to the US began on 24 May 1976 with a flight to <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">Washington Dulles</a> airport, and flights to <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">New York JFK</a> airport followed on 22 September 1977. Service to Singapore was established in co-operation with <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">Singapore Airlines</a> as a continuation of the flight to Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP7_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP7-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Air_France_Flight_4590" title="Air France Flight 4590">Air France Concorde crash</a> in Paris and a slump in air travel following the <a href="/wiki/11_September_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="11 September attacks">11 September attacks</a> in New York in 2001, it was decided to cease Concorde operations in 2003 after 27 years of service. The final commercial Concorde flight was BA002 from New York-JFK to London-Heathrow on 24 October 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP2000_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP2000-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1892" data-file-height="984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Trident_Three_G-AWZA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British Airways <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Trident" title="Hawker Siddeley Trident">Hawker Siddeley Trident</a> in its transitional scheme with BEA livery but with British Airways titles.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1981 the airline was instructed to prepare for privatisation by the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Thatcher</a> government. <a href="/wiki/John_King,_Baron_King_of_Wartnaby" title="John King, Baron King of Wartnaby">Sir John King</a>, later Lord King, was appointed chairman, charged with bringing the airline back into profitability. While many other large airlines struggled, King was credited with transforming British Airways into one of the most profitable air carriers in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The flag carrier was privatised and was floated on the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a> in February 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> British Airways effected the takeover of the UK\'s "second" airline, British Caledonian, in July of that same year.<sup id="cite_ref-BAP_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAP-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The formation of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic" title="Virgin Atlantic">Virgin Atlantic</a> in 1984 created a competitor for BA. The intense rivalry between British Airways and Virgin Atlantic culminated in the former being sued for libel in 1993, arising from claims and counterclaims over a <a href="/wiki/Dirty_Tricks_(British_Airways_scandal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dirty Tricks (British Airways scandal)">"dirty tricks" campaign</a> against Virgin. This campaign included allegations of poaching Virgin Atlantic customers, tampering with private files belonging to Virgin, and undermining Virgin\'s reputation in the city. As a result of the case BA management apologised "unreservedly", and the company agreed to pay \xc2\xa3110,000 damages to Virgin, \xc2\xa3500,000 to Branson personally and \xc2\xa33&#160;million legal costs.<sup id="cite_ref-dirtytricks_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirtytricks-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Lord King stepped down as chairman in 1993 and was replaced by his deputy, Colin Marshall, while <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ayling" title="Robert Ayling">Bob Ayling</a> took over as CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-EOP9_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP9-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Virgin filed a separate action in the US that same year regarding BA\'s domination of the trans-Atlantic routes, but it was thrown out in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-dirtytricks_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirtytricks-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1023" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Concorde_official_handover_ceremony_Fitzgerald.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Airways\' first <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Concorde</a> at Heathrow Airport, on 15 January 1976.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1992 British Airways expanded through the acquisition of the financially troubled <a href="/wiki/Dan-Air" title="Dan-Air">Dan-Air</a>, giving BA a much larger presence at <a href="/wiki/Gatwick_Airport" title="Gatwick Airport">Gatwick Airport</a>. <a href="/wiki/British_Asia_Airways" title="British Asia Airways">British Asia Airways</a>, a subsidiary based in Taiwan, was formed in March 1993 to operate between London and <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>. That same month BA purchased a 25% stake in the Australian airline <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a> and, with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Brymon_Airways" title="Brymon Airways">Brymon Airways</a> in May, formed British Airways Citiexpress (later <a href="/wiki/BA_Connect" title="BA Connect">BA Connect</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-EOP9_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP9-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In September 1998, British Airways, along with <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a>, Qantas, and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Airlines" title="Canadian Airlines">Canadian Airlines</a>, formed the <a href="/wiki/Oneworld" title="Oneworld">Oneworld</a> <a href="/wiki/Airline_alliance" title="Airline alliance">airline alliance</a>. Oneworld began operations on 1 February 1999, and is the third-largest airline alliance in the world, behind <a href="/wiki/SkyTeam" title="SkyTeam">SkyTeam</a> and Star Alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-BAP_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAP-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bob Ayling\'s leadership led to a cost savings of \xc2\xa3750m and the establishment of a budget airline, <a href="/wiki/Go_(airline)" title="Go (airline)">Go</a>, in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-aylingquits_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aylingquits-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The next year, however, British Airways reported an 84% drop in profits in its first quarter alone, its worst in seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In March 2000, Ayling was removed from his position and British Airways announced <a href="/wiki/Rod_Eddington" title="Rod Eddington">Rod Eddington</a> as his successor. That year, British Airways and <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a> conducted talks on a potential merger, reaching a decision in July to file an official merger plan with the European Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The plan fell through in September 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> British Asia Airways ceased operations in 2001 after BA suspended flights to Taipei. Go was sold to its management and the private equity firm <a href="/wiki/3i" title="3i">3i</a> in June 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Eddington would make further workforce cuts due to reduced demand following 11 September attacks in 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-EOP2000_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOP2000-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and BA sold its stake in Qantas in September 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In 2005 <a href="/wiki/Willie_Walsh_(Irish_businessman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Willie Walsh (Irish businessman)">Willie Walsh</a>, managing director of <a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer Lingus</a> and a former pilot, became the chief executive officer of British Airways.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> BA unveiled its new subsidiary <a href="/wiki/OpenSkies" title="OpenSkies">OpenSkies</a> in January 2008, taking advantage of the liberalisation of transatlantic traffic rights between Europe and the United States. OpenSkies flies non-stop from Paris to New York\'s JFK and Newark airports.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Iberia_aircraft_tails_BA_IB.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Airways and Iberia merged in January 2011, forming International Airlines Group, one of the world\'s largest airlines.</div></div></div>\n<p>In July 2008, British Airways announced a merger plan with <a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a>, another flag carrier airline in the Oneworld alliance, wherein each airline would retain its original brand.<sup id="cite_ref-baiberiamerger_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baiberiamerger-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The agreement was confirmed in April 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-BAIM_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAIM-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and in July the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">US Department of Transport</a> permitted the merger and began to co-ordinate <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_flight" title="Transatlantic flight">transatlantic</a> routes with American Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-ECABAIB_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECABAIB-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DAOAIA_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DAOAIA-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> On 6 October 2010 the alliance between British Airways, American Airlines and Iberia formally began operations. The alliance generates an estimated \xc2\xa3230&#160;million in annual cost-saving for BA, in addition to the \xc2\xa3330&#160;million which would be saved by the merger with Iberia.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This merger was finalised on 21 January 2011, resulting in the International Airlines Group (IAG), the world\'s third-largest airline in terms of annual revenue and the second-largest airline group in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-BAIM_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAIM-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Prior to merging, British Airways owned a 13.5% stake in Iberia, and thus received ownership of 55% of the combined International Airlines Group; Iberia\'s other shareholders received the remaining 45%.<sup id="cite_ref-TEP_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TEP-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> As a part of the merger, British Airways ceased trading independently on the London Stock Exchange after 23 years as a constituent of the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100_Index" title="FTSE 100 Index">FTSE 100 Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2010 Willie Walsh, now CEO of IAG, announced that the group was considering acquiring other airlines and had drawn up a shortlist of twelve possible acquisitions.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In November 2011 IAG announced an agreement in principle to purchase British Midland International from <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> A contract to purchase the airline was agreed the next month,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and the sale was completed for \xc2\xa3172.5 million on 30 March 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The airline established a new subsidiary based at <a href="/wiki/London_City_Airport" title="London City Airport">London City Airport</a> operating <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A318" title="Airbus A318">Airbus A318s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways was the official airline partner of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">London 2012 Olympic Games</a>. On 18 May 2012 it flew the Olympic flame from <a href="/wiki/Athens_International_Airport" title="Athens International Airport">Athens International Airport</a> to <a href="/wiki/RNAS_Culdrose_(HMS_Seahawk)" title="RNAS Culdrose (HMS Seahawk)">RNAS Culdrose</a> while carrying various dignitaries, including Lord <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Coe" title="Sebastian Coe">Sebastian Coe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Anne" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Anne">Princess Anne</a>, the Olympics minister <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Robertson_(politician)" title="Hugh Robertson (politician)">Hugh Robertson</a> and the London Mayor <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>, along with the footballer <a href="/wiki/David_Beckham" title="David Beckham">David Beckham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 27 May 2017, British Airways suffered a computer power failure. All flights were cancelled and thousands of passengers were affected.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By the following day, the company had not succeeded in reestablishing the normal function of their computer systems. When asked by reporters for more information on the ongoing problems, British Airways stated "The root cause was a power supply issue which our affected our IT systems - we continue to investigate this" and declined to comment further.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Willie_Walsh_(businessman)" title="Willie Walsh (businessman)">Willie Walsh</a> later attributed the crash to an electrical engineer disconnecting the <a href="/wiki/Uninterruptible_power_supply" title="Uninterruptible power supply">UPS</a> and said there would be an independent investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Amidst the decline in the value of Iranian currency due to the reintroduction of US sanctions on Iran, BA announced that the Iranian route is "not commercially viable". As a result, BA decided to stop their services in Iran, effective 22 September 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2019, as part of the celebrations of a centenary of airline operations in the United Kingdom, British Airways announced that four aircraft would receive retro liveries. The first of these is a Boeing 747-400 (G-BYGC), which was repainted into the former BOAC livery, which it retained until its retirement. Two more Boeing 747-400s were repainted with former British Airways liveries. One wore the "<a href="/wiki/Landor_Associates" title="Landor Associates">Landor</a>" livery until its retirement in 2020 (G-BNLY), the other (G-CIVB), wore the original "Union Jack" livery until its retirement in 2020 also. An Airbus A319 was repainted into <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">British European Airways</a> livery, which is still flying as G-EUPJ.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight455845_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight455845-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 April 2020, the company set out plans to make up to 12,000 staff redundant because of the <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_2019%E2%80%9320_coronavirus_pandemic_on_aviation" class="mw-redirect" title="Impact of the 2019\xe2\x80\x9320 coronavirus pandemic on aviation">global collapse of air traffic due to the COVID-19 pandemic</a><sup id="cite_ref-guardian-british-airways-plans-to-make-up-to-12000-staff-redundant_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-british-airways-plans-to-make-up-to-12000-staff-redundant-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and that it may not reopen its operations at Gatwick airport.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In July 2020, British Airways announced the immediate retirement of their entire 747-400 fleet, having originally intended to phase out the remaining 747s in 2024. The airline stated that their decision to bring forward the date was in part due to the downturn in air travel following the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a> pandemic and to focus on incorporating more modern and fuel-efficient aircraft such as the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A350" title="Airbus A350">Airbus A350</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 787">Boeing 787</a>. At the same time, British Airways also announced its intention to completely eliminate carbon emissions by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> On 28 July 2020, the company\'s cabin crew union issued an "industrial action" warning in order to prevent the 12,000 job cuts and pay cuts.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 12 October 2020, it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Sean_Doyle_(businessman)" title="Sean Doyle (businessman)">Sean Doyle</a>, CEO of Aer Lingus (also part of the IAG airline group) would succeed <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lex_Cruz_(businessman)" title="\xc3\x81lex Cruz (businessman)">\xc3\x81lex Cruz</a> as CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_affairs">Corporate affairs</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Airways_destinations" title="List of British Airways destinations">List of British Airways destinations</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Airways_franchise_destinations" title="List of British Airways franchise destinations">List of British Airways franchise destinations</a></div>\n<p>British Airways is the largest airline based in the United Kingdom in terms of fleet size, international flights, and international destinations and was, until 2008, the largest airline by passenger numbers. The airline carried 34.6&#160;million passengers in 2008, but, rival carrier <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">easyJet</a> transported 44.5&#160;million passengers that year, passing British Airways for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-British_Airways_Traffic_Statistics_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_Airways_Traffic_Statistics-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Passenger_statistics_for_December_2008_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Passenger_statistics_for_December_2008-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> British Airways holds a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Aviation Authority (United Kingdom)">United Kingdom Civil Aviation Authority</a> Type A Operating Licence, it is permitted to carry passengers, cargo, and mail on aircraft with 20 or more seats.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg/170px-WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg/255px-WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg/340px-WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2370" data-file-height="3160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WatersideBAHQLondon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Waterside_(building)" title="Waterside (building)">Waterside</a>, the head office building of British Airways.</div></div></div>\n<p>The airlines\' head office, <a href="/wiki/Waterside_(building)" title="Waterside (building)">Waterside</a>, stands in <a href="/wiki/Harmondsworth" title="Harmondsworth">Harmondsworth</a>, a village that is near Heathrow Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Waterside was completed in June 1998 to replace British Airways\' previous head office, Speedbird House,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> located in Technical Block C on the grounds of Heathrow.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways\' main base is at Heathrow Airport, but it also has a major presence at Gatwick Airport. It also has a base at London City Airport, where its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/BA_Cityflyer" class="mw-redirect" title="BA Cityflyer">BA Cityflyer</a> is the largest operator. BA had previously operated a significant hub at <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport" title="Manchester Airport">Manchester Airport</a>. Manchester to New York (JFK) services were withdrawn; later all international services outside London ceased when the subsidiary <a href="/wiki/BA_Connect" title="BA Connect">BA Connect</a> was sold. Passengers wishing to travel internationally with BA either to or from regional UK destinations must now transfer in London.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Heathrow Airport is dominated by British Airways, which owns 40% of the slots available at the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-slotsref_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slotsref-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The majority of BA services operate from <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Terminal_5" title="Heathrow Terminal 5">Terminal 5</a>, with the exception of some flights at <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Terminal_3" title="Heathrow Terminal 3">Terminal 3</a> owing to insufficient capacity at Terminal 5.\n</p><p>In August 2014, Willie Walsh advised the airline would continue to use flight paths over Iraq despite the hostilities there. A few days earlier Qantas announced it would avoid Iraqi airspace, while other airlines did likewise. The issue arose following the downing of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia_Airlines_Flight_17" title="Malaysia Airlines Flight 17">Malaysia Airlines Flight 17</a> over Ukraine, and a temporary suspension of flights to and from <a href="/wiki/Ben_Gurion_Airport" title="Ben Gurion Airport">Ben Gurion Airport</a> during the <a href="/wiki/2014_Israel%E2%80%93Gaza_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict">2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IraqBA_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IraqBA-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidiaries_and_shareholdings">Subsidiaries and shareholdings</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/BA_CityFlyer" title="BA CityFlyer">BA CityFlyer</a>, a wholly owned subsidiary, offers flights from its base at London City Airport to 23 destinations throughout Europe. It flies 22 <a href="/wiki/Embraer" title="Embraer">Embraer</a> E-190 aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The airline focuses on serving the financial market, though it has recently expanded into the leisure market, offering routes to Ibiza, Palma and Venice.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2015, <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a> purchased a 10% stake in <a href="/wiki/International_Airlines_Group" title="International Airlines Group">International Airlines Group</a>, the parent of British Airways and <a href="/wiki/Iberia_(airline)" title="Iberia (airline)">Iberia</a>, for \xe2\x82\xac1.2 billion (US$1.26 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> By early 2020, this had increased to 25% costing a further US$600 million.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>BEA Helicopters was renamed <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_Helicopters" title="British Airways Helicopters">British Airways Helicopters</a> in 1974 and operated passenger and offshore oil support services until it was sold in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Other former subsidiaries include the German airline <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_BA" class="mw-redirect" title="Deutsche BA">Deutsche BA</a> from 1997 until 2003 and the French airline <a href="/wiki/Air_Libert%C3%A9" title="Air Libert\xc3\xa9">Air Libert\xc3\xa9</a> from 1997 to 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> British Airways also owned Airways Aero Association, the operator of the <a href="/wiki/Airways_Flying_Club" title="Airways Flying Club">British Airways flying club</a> based at <a href="/wiki/Wycombe_Air_Park" title="Wycombe Air Park">Wycombe Air Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/High_Wycombe" title="High Wycombe">High Wycombe</a>, until it was sold to <a href="/wiki/Surinder_Arora" title="Surinder Arora">Surinder Arora</a> in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>South Africa\'s <a href="/wiki/Comair_(South_Africa)" title="Comair (South Africa)">Comair</a> and Denmark\'s <a href="/wiki/Sun_Air_of_Scandinavia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sun Air of Scandinavia">Sun Air of Scandinavia</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Franchisee" class="mw-redirect" title="Franchisee">franchisees</a> of British Airways since 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> British Airways obtained a 15% stake in UK regional airline <a href="/wiki/Flybe" title="Flybe">Flybe</a> from the sale of BA Connect in March 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> It sold the stake in 2014. BA also owned a 10% stake in InterCapital and Regional Rail (ICRR), the company that managed the operations of <a href="/wiki/Eurostar_International_Limited" title="Eurostar International Limited">Eurostar (UK) Ltd</a> from 1998 to 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> when the management of Eurostar was restructured.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg/220px-2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg/330px-2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg/440px-2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3087" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2010-06-30_B757_OpenSkies_F-HAVN_EDDF_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/OpenSkies" title="OpenSkies">OpenSkies</a> Boeing 757-200 landing at <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Airport" title="Frankfurt Airport">Frankfurt Airport</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>With the creation of an <a href="/wiki/EU%E2%80%93US_Open_Skies_Agreement" title="EU\xe2\x80\x93US Open Skies Agreement">Open Skies agreement</a> between Europe and the United States in March 2008, British Airways started a new subsidiary airline called <a href="/wiki/OpenSkies" title="OpenSkies">OpenSkies</a> (previously known as "Project Lauren").<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The airline started operations in June 2008, and flew directly from Paris\xe2\x80\x94Orly to Newark.<sup id="cite_ref-os13_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-os13-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> However it ceased operations on 2 September 2018 when it was replaced with <a href="/wiki/Level_(airline)" class="mw-redirect" title="Level (airline)">Level</a> flights on that route.<sup id="cite_ref-iagofficial_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iagofficial-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/British_Airways_Ltd_(2012%E2%80%9315)" title="British Airways Ltd (2012\xe2\x80\x9315)">British Airways Limited</a> was established in 2012 to take over the operation of the premium service between London City Airport and New York-JFK. BA began the service in September 2009, using two Airbus A318s fitted with 32 lie-flat beds in an all business class cabin.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Flights operate under the numbers previously reserved for Concorde: BA001 \xe2\x80\x93 BA004.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The flights returned to be directly operated by British Airways plc in 2015.\n</p><p>British Airways provides cargo services under the <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_World_Cargo" title="British Airways World Cargo">British Airways World Cargo</a> brand. The division has been part of <a href="/wiki/IAG_Cargo" title="IAG Cargo">IAG Cargo</a> since 2012 and is the world\'s twelfth-largest <a href="/wiki/Cargo_airline" title="Cargo airline">cargo airline</a> based on total freight tonne-kilometres flown.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> BA World Cargo operates using the main BA fleet. Until the end of March 2014 they also operated three <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-8" title="Boeing 747-8">Boeing 747-8</a> freighter aircraft providing dedicated long-haul services under a <a href="/wiki/Wet_lease" class="mw-redirect" title="Wet lease">wet lease</a> arrangement from <a href="/wiki/Global_Supply_Systems" title="Global Supply Systems">Global Supply Systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The division operates an automated cargo centre at Heathrow Airport and handles freight at Gatwick and Stansted airports.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_trends">Business trends</span></h3>\n<p>The key trends for the British Airways Plc Group are shown below.\n</p><p>On the merger with Iberia, the accounting reference date was changed from 31 March to 31 December; figures below are therefore for the years to 31 March up to 2010, for the nine months to 31 December 2010, and for the years to href="#cite_note-Report2011-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><sup id="cite_ref-Report2013_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report2013-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><sup id="cite_ref-Report2014_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report2014-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:left;"><small> *After deconsolidation <br /> of AGL</small><sup id="cite_ref-Report2015_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report2015-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In 2020, due to the crisis caused by the COVID-19 pandemic, British Airways had to reduce its&#160;42,000-strong workforce by 12,000 jobs. According to the estimate by IAG, a parent company, it will take the air travel industry several years to return to previous performance and profitability levels.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_relations">Industrial relations</span></h3>\n<p>Staff working for British Airways are represented by a number of trade unions, pilots are represented by <a href="/wiki/British_Air_Line_Pilots%27_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="British Air Line Pilots&#39; Association">British Air Line Pilots\' Association</a>, cabin crew by <a href="/wiki/British_Airlines_Stewards_and_Stewardesses_Association" title="British Airlines Stewards and Stewardesses Association">British Airlines Stewards and Stewardesses Association</a> (a branch of <a href="/wiki/Unite_the_Union" title="Unite the Union">Unite the Union</a>), while other branches of Unite the Union and the <a href="/wiki/GMB_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="GMB Union">GMB Union</a> represent other employees. Bob Ayling\'s management faced strike action by cabin crew over a \xc2\xa31 billion cost-cutting drive to return BA to profitability in 1997; this was the last time BA cabin crew would strike until 2009, although staff morale has reportedly been unstable since that incident.<sup id="cite_ref-CCSA_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCSA-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In an effort to increase interaction between management, employees, and the unions, various conferences and workshops have taken place, often with thousands in attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, wildcat action was taken by union members over a decision by <a href="/wiki/Gate_Gourmet" class="mw-redirect" title="Gate Gourmet">Gate Gourmet</a> not to renew the contracts of 670 workers and replace them with agency staff; it is estimated that the strike cost British Airways \xc2\xa330&#160;million and caused disruption to 100,000 passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In October 2006, BA became involved in a <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_cross_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="British Airways cross controversy">civil rights dispute</a> when a Christian employee was forbidden to wear a necklace bearing the <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a>, a religious symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> BA\'s practice of forbidding such symbols has been publicly questioned by British politicians such as the former <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Reid,_Baron_Reid_of_Cardowan" title="John Reid, Baron Reid of Cardowan">John Reid</a> and the former <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Relations have been turbulent between BA and Unite. In 2007, cabin crew threatened strike action over salary changes to be imposed by BA management. The strike was called off at the last minute, British Airways losing \xc2\xa380&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-CCSA_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CCSA-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In December 2009, a ballot for strike action over Christmas received a high level of support,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> action was blocked by a court injunction that deemed the ballot illegal. Negotiations failed to stop strike action in March, BA withdrew perks for strike participants.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Allegations were made by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> newspaper that BA had consulted outside firms methods to undermine the unions: the story was later withdrawn.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> A strike was announced for May 2010, British Airways again sought an injunction. Members of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Workers_Party_(UK)" title="Socialist Workers Party (UK)">Socialist Workers Party</a> disrupted negotiations between BA management and Unite to prevent industrial action.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Further disruption struck when <a href="/wiki/Derek_Simpson_(trade_unionist)" title="Derek Simpson (trade unionist)">Derek Simpson</a>, a Unite co-leader, was discovered to have leaked details of confidential negotiations online via Twitter.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Industrial action re-emerged in 2017, this time by BA\'s Mixed Fleet flight attendants, whom were employed on much less favorable pay and terms and conditions compared to previous cabin staff who joined prior to 2010. A ballot for industrial action was distributed to Mixed Fleet crew in November 2016<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and resulted in an overwhelming yes majority for industrial action.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Unite described Mixed Fleet crew as on "poverty pay", with many Mixed Fleet flight attendants sleeping in their cars in between shifts because they cannot afford the fuel to drive home, or operating while sick as they cannot afford to call in sick and lose their pay for the shift. Unite also blasted BA of removing staff travel concessions, bonus payments and other benefits to all cabin crew who undertook industrial action, as well as strike-breaking tactics such as wet-leasing aircraft from other airlines and offering financial incentives for cabin crew not to strike.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The first dates of strikes during Christmas 2016 were cancelled due to pay negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Industrial action by Mixed Fleet commenced in January 2017 after rejecting a pay offer.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Strike action continued throughout 2017 in numerous discontinuous periods, resulting in one of the longest running disputes in aviation history.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> On 31 October 2017, after 85 days of discontinuous industrial action, Mixed Fleet accepted a new pay deal from BA which ended the dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senior_Leadership">Senior Leadership</span></h3>\n<ul><li><i>Chairman:</i> <a serves over 160 destinations, including eight domestic and 26 in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliances">Alliances</span></h3>\n<p>British Airways is a member and one of the founders of Oneworld, an airline alliance.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Codeshare_agreements">Codeshare agreements</span></h3>\n<p>British Airways <a href="/wiki/Codeshare_agreement" title="Codeshare agreement">codeshares</a> with the following airlines:<sup id="cite_ref-CAPA_BA_profile_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAPA_BA_profile-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 17em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aer_Lingus" title="Aer Lingus">Aer title="Vueling">Vueling</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fleet">Fleet</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_fleet" title="British Airways fleet">British Airways fleet</a></div>\n<p>As of October&#160;2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=British_Airways&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the British Airways operates a fleet of 249 aircraft with 55 orders. BA operates a mix of <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> narrow and wide-body aircraft, and <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> wide-body aircraft, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">777</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">787</a>. In October 2020, British Airways retired its fleet of 747-400 aircraft. <sup id="cite_ref-747_retirement_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-747_retirement-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> It was one of the largest operators of the 747, having previously operated the -100, -200, and -400 aircraft from 1974 (1969 with <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">BOAC</a>). Prior to the introduction of the 787, when Boeing built an aircraft for British Airways, it was allocated the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_customer_codes" title="List of Boeing customer codes">customer code</a> <i>36</i>, which appeared in their aircraft designation as a suffix, such as 737-436.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Airways_Engineering">British Airways Engineering</span></h3>\n<p>The airline has its own <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_Engineering" title="British Airways Engineering">engineering branch</a> to maintain its aircraft fleet, this includes line maintenance at over 70 airports around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> As well as hangar facilities at Heathrow and Gatwick airport it has two major maintenance centres at <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Airport" title="Glasgow Airport">Glasgow</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_Maintenance_Cardiff" title="British Airways Maintenance Cardiff">Cardiff Airports</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branding">Branding</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg/220px-British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg/330px-British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg/440px-British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_at_Terminal_5_in_2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Airways\' promotional banner above the check-in desks at Heathrow Airport\'s <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Terminal_5" title="Heathrow Terminal 5">Terminal 5</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png/220px-Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png/330px-Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png/440px-Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Typeface_sample_Mylius_Modern.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mylius Modern, a custom-made typeface used by British Airways.</div></div></div>\n<p>The musical theme predominantly used on British Airways advertising is <i>"<a href="/wiki/The_Flower_Duet" class="mw-redirect" title="The Flower Duet">The Flower Duet</a>"</i> by <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9o_Delibes" title="L\xc3\xa9o Delibes">L\xc3\xa9o Delibes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> This, and the slogan "The World\'s Favourite Airline" were introduced in 1989 with the launch of the iconic <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_face_advertisement" title="British Airways face advertisement">"Face" advertisement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The slogan was dropped in 2001 after <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a> overtook BA in terms of passenger numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> "Flower Duet" is still used by the airline, and has been through several different arrangements since 1989. The most recent version of this melody was shown in 2007 with a new slogan: "Upgrade to British Airways".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Other advertising slogans have included "The World\'s Best Airline", "We\'ll Take More Care of You", and "Fly the Flag".<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>BA had an account for 23 years with <a href="/wiki/Saatchi_%26_Saatchi" title="Saatchi &amp; Saatchi">Saatchi &amp; Saatchi</a>, an agency that created many of their most famous advertisements, including the influential "Face" campaign. Saatchi &amp; Saatchi later imitated this advert for <a href="/wiki/Silverjet" title="Silverjet">Silverjet</a>, a rival of BA, after BA discontinued their business activities.<sup id="cite_ref-SJA_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJA-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Since 2007, BA has used <a href="/wiki/Bartle_Bogle_Hegarty" title="Bartle Bogle Hegarty">Bartle Bogle Hegarty</a> as its advertising agency.<sup id="cite_ref-adref_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adref-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British Airways purchased the <a href="/wiki/Internet_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet domain">internet domain</a> ba.com in 2002 from previous owner <a href="/wiki/Bell_Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Atlantic">Bell Atlantic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> \'BA\' being the company\'s acronym and its <a href="/wiki/International_Air_Transport_Association" title="International Air Transport Association">IATA</a> Airline code.\n</p><p>British Airways is the official airline of the <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon Championship tennis tournament</a>, and was the official airline and tier one partner of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a> and <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">Paralympics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> British Airways was also the official airline of England\'s bid to host the <a href="/wiki/2018_and_2022_FIFA_World_Cup_bids" title="2018 and 2022 FIFA World Cup bids">2018 Football World Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>High Life</i>, founded in 1973, is the official in-flight magazine of the airline.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_video">Safety video</span></h3>\n<p>The airline used a cartoon safety video from circa 2005 until 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Beginning on 1 September 2017 the airline introduced the new <a href="/wiki/Comic_Relief" title="Comic Relief">Comic Relief</a> live action safety video hosted by <a href="/wiki/Chabuddy_G" class="mw-redirect" title="Chabuddy G">Chabuddy G</a>, with appearances by British celebrities <a href="/wiki/Gillian_Anderson" title="Gillian Anderson">Gillian Anderson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Loughrey_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loughrey-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Atkinson" title="Rowan Atkinson">Rowan Atkinson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jim_Broadbent" title="Jim Broadbent">Jim Broadbent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rob_Brydon" title="Rob Brydon">Rob Brydon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Davis" title="Warwick Davis">Warwick Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiwetel_Ejiofor" title="Chiwetel Ejiofor">Chiwetel Ejiofor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_McKellen" title="Ian McKellen">Ian McKellen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thandie_Newton" class="mw-redirect" title="Thandie Newton">Thandie Newton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Ramsay" title="Gordon Ramsay">Gordon Ramsay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Loughrey_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loughrey-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> A "sequel" video, also hosted by Chabuddy G, was released in 2018, with <a href="/wiki/Michael_Caine" title="Michael Caine">Michael Caine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Colman" title="Olivia Colman">Olivia Colman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jourdan_Dunn" title="Jourdan Dunn">Jourdan Dunn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naomie_Harris" title="Naomie Harris">Naomie Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joanna_Lumley" title="Joanna Lumley">Joanna Lumley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Walliams" title="David Walliams">David Walliams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The two videos are part of Comic Relief\'s charity programme.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Liveries.2C_logos.2C_and_tail_fins"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Liveries,_logos,_and_tail_fins">Liveries, logos, and tail fins</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_British_Airways#Liveries_and_logos" title="History of British Airways">History of British Airways \xc2\xa7&#160;Liveries and logos</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_(Landor_livery)_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_(City_of_Swansea)_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/British_Airways_%28Landor_livery%29_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_%28City_of_Swansea%29_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg/220px-British_Airways_%28Landor_livery%29_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_%28City_of_Swansea%29_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/British_Airways_%28Landor_livery%29_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_%28City_of_Swansea%29_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg/330px-British_Airways_%28Landor_livery%29_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_%28City_of_Swansea%29_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/British_Airways_%28Landor_livery%29_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_%28City_of_Swansea%29_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg/440px-British_Airways_%28Landor_livery%29_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_%28City_of_Swansea%29_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_(Landor_livery)_Boeing_747-436_G-BNLY_(City_of_Swansea)_departing_JFK_Airport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the four retro liveries to celebrate the 100th anniversary of British Airways and its predecessors. This <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-400" title="Boeing 747-400">Boeing 747-400</a> aircraft (registered as G-BNLY) is painted in the <a href="/wiki/Landor_Associates" title="Landor Associates">Landor Associates</a> design.</div></div></div>\n<p>The aeroplanes that British Airways inherited from the four-way merger between BOAC, BEA, Cambrian, and Northeast were temporarily given the text logo "British airways" but retained the original airline\'s livery. With its formation in 1974, British Airways\' aeroplanes were given a new white, blue, and red colour scheme with a stylized <a href="/wiki/Union_Jack" title="Union Jack">Union Jack</a> painted on their tail fins, designed by Negus &amp; Negus. In 1984, a new livery designed by <a href="/wiki/Landor_Associates" title="Landor Associates">Landor Associates</a> updated the airline\'s look as it prepared for privatization.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> For celebrating centenary, BA announced four retro liveries...three on Boeing 747-400 aircraft (one in each of BOAC, Negus &amp; Negus, and Landor Associates liveries), and one A319 in BEA livery.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/British_Airways_ethnic_liveries" title="British Airways ethnic liveries">British Airways ethnic liveries</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_(27078321943).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/British_Airways_%2827078321943%29.jpg/220px-British_Airways_%2827078321943%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/British_Airways_%2827078321943%29.jpg/330px-British_Airways_%2827078321943%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/British_Airways_%2827078321943%29.jpg/440px-British_Airways_%2827078321943%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3120" data-file-height="1991" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_(27078321943).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Current BA aircraft bear <i>Chatham Dockyard Union flag</i> tail art.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1997, there was a controversial change to a new Project Utopia livery; all aircraft used the corporate colours consistently on the fuselage, but tailfins bore one of multiple designs.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Several people spoke out against the change, including the former prime minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>, who famously covered the tail of a model 747 at an event with a handkerchief, to show her displeasure.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> BA\'s traditional rival, Virgin Atlantic, took advantage of the negative press coverage by applying the Union flag to the winglets of their aircraft along with the slogan "Britain\'s national flagcarrier".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1999, the CEO of British Airways, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Ayling" class="mw-redirect" title="Bob Ayling">Bob Ayling</a>, announced that all BA planes would adopt the tailfin design <i>Chatham Dockyard Union Flag</i> originally intended to be used only on the Concorde, based on the Union Flag.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> All BA aircraft have since borne the <i>Chatham Dockyard Union flag</i> variant of the Project Utopia livery, except for the four retro aircraft.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loyalty_programmes">Loyalty programmes</span></h3>\n<p>British Airways\' tiered loyalty programme, called the Executive Club, includes access to special lounges and dedicated "fast" queues.<sup id="cite_ref-execclub_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-execclub-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> BA also invites its top corporate accounts to join a "Premier" incentive programme. British Airways operates airside lounges for passengers travelling in premium cabins, and these are available to certain tiers of Executive Club members.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> First class passengers, as well as Gold Executive Club members, are entitled to use First Class Lounges. Business class passengers (called Club World or Club Europe in BA terms) as well as Silver Executive Club members may use Business lounges.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> At airports in which BA does not operate a departure lounge, a third party lounge is often provided for premium or status passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, due to the merger with Iberia, British Airways announced changes to the Executive Club to maximise integration between the airlines. This included the combination and rebranding of <a href="/wiki/Air_Miles#Air_Miles_United_Kingdom" title="Air Miles">Air Miles</a>, BA Miles and Iberia Plus points as the IAG operated loyalty programme <a href="/wiki/Avios" class="mw-redirect" title="Avios">Avios</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflight_magazines">Inflight magazines</span></h3>\n<p><i><b>high life Magazine</b></i> is British Airways\' complimentary inflight magazine. It is available to all customers across all cabins and aircraft types.\n</p><p><i><b>high life shop Magazine</b></i> is British Airways\' inflight shopping magazine. It is available to all customers on all aircraft where the inflight shopping range can be carried.\n</p><p><i><b>First life</b></i> is a complimentary magazine offered to all customers travelling in the First cabin. It has a range of articles including fashion, trends and technology with an upmarket target audience.\n</p><p><i><b>Business life</b></i> is a complimentary magazine targeted at business travellers and frequent flyers. The magazine can be found in all short haul aircraft seat pockets, in the magazine selection for Club World customers and in lounges operated by British Airways.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabins_and_services">Cabins and services</span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">British Airways\' <a href="/wiki/Economy_class" title="Economy class">Euro Traveller</a> cabin, installed on all of the airline\'s narrow-body aircraft.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:BA_New_Club_World_seat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/BA_New_Club_World_seat.jpg/220px-BA_New_Club_World_seat.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="188"
